  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_82449f20-3aff-4f6f-8b99-686168e6bdd9'

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer5517022125145025147">Considered the high point of Gogol's writing for the stage and a masterpiece of dramatic satire, <i>The Inspector General</i> skewers the stupidity, greed, and venality of Russian provincial officials. When it is announced that the Inspector General is coming to visit incognito, Anton, the chief of police, hastens to clean up the town before his arrival. Local officials scurry to</span>
  <span id="freeText5517022125145025147" style="display:none">Considered the high point of Gogol's writing for the stage and a masterpiece of dramatic satire, <i>The Inspector General</i> skewers the stupidity, greed, and venality of Russian provincial officials. When it is announced that the Inspector General is coming to visit incognito, Anton, the chief of police, hastens to clean up the town before his arrival. Local officials scurry to hide evidence of bribe-taking and other misdeeds, setting the stage for the arrival from St. Petersburg of Ivan, a penurious gambler and rake who is promptly taken by the townspeople to be the dreaded Inspector General. Ivan, and his servant, Osip, soon take advantage of the situation with hilarious results. First performed in 1836, the play transcends regional and national boundaries to offer a biting, highly entertaining glimpse of universal human foibles and failings.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5517022125145025147" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

<div id="review_1421596309" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1421596309" />
    <a title="Ahmad Sharabiani" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/614778-ahmad-sharabiani"><img alt="Ahmad Sharabiani" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1194930432p2/614778.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1421596309?book_show_action=true">Oct 20, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ahmad Sharabiani" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ahmad Sharabiani" href="/user/show/614778-ahmad-sharabiani">Ahmad Sharabiani</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1421596309" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3201000067029164233">Ревизор = The Inspector General = The Government Inspector, Nikolai Gogol <br/><br/>The Government Inspector, is a satirical play by the Russian and Ukrainian dramatist and novelist Nikolai Gogol. Originally published in 1836. <br/><br/>The play is a comedy of errors, satirizing human greed, stupidity, and the extensive political corruption of Imperial Russia.<br/><br/>The corrupt officials of a small Russian town, headed by the Mayor, react with terror to the news that an incognito inspector (the incognito one) will soon be</span>
  <span id="freeText3201000067029164233" style="display:none">Ревизор = The Inspector General = The Government Inspector, Nikolai Gogol <br /><br />The Government Inspector, is a satirical play by the Russian and Ukrainian dramatist and novelist Nikolai Gogol. Originally published in 1836. <br /><br />The play is a comedy of errors, satirizing human greed, stupidity, and the extensive political corruption of Imperial Russia.<br /><br />The corrupt officials of a small Russian town, headed by the Mayor, react with terror to the news that an incognito inspector (the incognito one) will soon be arriving in their town to investigate them. The flurry of activity to cover up their considerable misdeeds is interrupted by the report that a suspicious person has arrived two weeks previously from Saint Petersburg and is staying at the inn. That person, however, is not an inspector; it is Khlestakov, a foppish civil servant with a wild imagination.<br /><br />Having learned that Khlestakov has been charging his considerable hotel bill to the Crown, the Mayor and his crooked cronies are immediately certain that this upper-class twit is the dreaded inspector. For quite some time, however, Khlestakov does not even realize that he has been mistaken for someone else. Meanwhile, he enjoys the officials' terrified deference and moves in as a guest in the Mayor's house. He also demands and receives massive "loans" from the Mayor and all of his associates. He also flirts outrageously with the Mayor's wife and daughter. ...<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: بیست و یکم ماه اکتبر سال 1977 میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: بازرس؛ نیکلای گوگول؛ مترجم: محمدرضا نیک بخش؛ تهران، بامداد، 1355، در 192 ص؛ موضوع: نمایشنامه های نویسندگان روس - سده 19 م<br /><br />عنوان: بازرس - کمدی در پنج پرده؛ نیکلای گوگول؛ مترجم: آبتین گلکار؛ تهران، هرمس، 1388، در 147 ص؛ شابک: 9789643636029؛<br /><br />نمایشنامه ی «بازرس»، با خبر آمدن یک بازرس، به شهری دورافتاده، آغاز می‌شود. خبر، مسئولان ادارات را، به تکاپو می‌اندازد، تا برای نابسامانیها و تخلفات خود، و همقطارانشان، تدبیری بیندیشند. مردمان شهر نیز، به چالش می‌افتند، تا نارضایتی خویش از شهردار شهر خویش را، به گوش بازرس برسانند. و ...؛ ا. شربیانی</span>

<div id="review_3568576317" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3568576317" />
    <a title="Piyangie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48056505-piyangie"><img alt="Piyangie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1590579939p2/48056505.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3568576317?book_show_action=true">Sep 29, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Piyangie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Piyangie" href="/user/show/48056505-piyangie">Piyangie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/17996772-the-inspector-general">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48056505-piyangie?shelf=russian-lit">russian-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48056505-piyangie?shelf=plays">plays</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3568576317" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7826936149692099981"><i> The Inspector General </i> by Gogol is one of the best satirical plays that I've read. Gogol is well known for his satire, either subtle or presumptuous. Satire is his forte. And in <i> The Inspector General</i>, it is expressed in its best. <br/><br/>Gogol voiced strongly against the greed, corruption, and inefficiency of the government officials. His various works are proof of this. But in my view, it is in <i>The Inspector General</i>, that Gogol discloses his views strongly.<br/><br/>The story is set in a provincial town, and i</span>
  <span id="freeText7826936149692099981" style="display:none"><i> The Inspector General </i> by Gogol is one of the best satirical plays that I've read. Gogol is well known for his satire, either subtle or presumptuous. Satire is his forte. And in <i> The Inspector General</i>, it is expressed in its best. <br /><br />Gogol voiced strongly against the greed, corruption, and inefficiency of the government officials. His various works are proof of this. But in my view, it is in <i>The Inspector General</i>, that Gogol discloses his views strongly.<br /><br />The story is set in a provincial town, and it exposes the greed, corruption, inefficiency, and the despotism of the local officials on an official scale, and also vanity, self-importance, and opportunism on a personal scale. These officials squander government money which is granted to improve the facilities in the town. They make the life of the people miserable by demanding exorbitant bribes for work to be done or for trade to be carried on and harass them unnecessarily just to show their power. And when the news of an official visit from Petersburg reaches their ears, these officials concoct a joint venture on how to deceive him. Could they deceive him or would they be deceived in turn?<br /><br />The play conveys a powerful message and the satire is presumptuous. The comical tone that is so perfectly maintained is most entertaining. Despite the gravity of the story, it was altogether hilarious. I can't remember the last time I've laughed over a play this much. It was so entertaining. Had I watched a performance, I'm sure to have died of laughter!<br /><br />Gogol has expressed his views so boldly through the play that while it won him critical acclaim it also incurred severe disapproval of the "subjects" of his play that he was compelled to leave Russia. That in itself is proof of its success. <br /><br /><i>The Inspector General</i> is undoubtedly one of the best works by Gogol, seconding only to <i> Dead Souls</i>, and as I've already said one of the best satirical plays that have come across me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7826936149692099981" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2239120326" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2239120326" />
    <a title="WhatIReallyRead" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/52500467-whatireallyread"><img alt="WhatIReallyRead" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1483674713p2/52500467.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2239120326?book_show_action=true">Jul 12, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="WhatIReallyRead" class="user" itemprop="url" name="WhatIReallyRead" href="/user/show/52500467-whatireallyread">WhatIReallyRead</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52500467-whatireallyread?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52500467-whatireallyread?shelf=play">play</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2239120326" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6134654650579451008">This play is satire at its best. Corruption, greed, vanity, opportunism, and snobbism are comically displayed. And before the spectator has a chance to get on a high horse, Gogol reminds us that these characters are taken from real life and each of us has some of the same vices. The writing is brilliant, astute, and a pleasure to read. I haven't seen this play performed.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_924922364" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/924922364" />
    <a title="David Sarkies" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5856247-david-sarkies"><img alt="David Sarkies" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1470027282p2/5856247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/924922364?book_show_action=true">Apr 30, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Sarkies" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Sarkies" href="/user/show/5856247-david-sarkies">David Sarkies</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">People wanting a good laugh</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5856247-david-sarkies?shelf=comedy">comedy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer924922364" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10950761547058553325"><b>Corrupting the Official</b><br/>3 March 2014<br/><br/>	To start off I feel that suggesting that Gogol is a Russian would be a bit of an insult to the Ukrainians, especially at this point in time, since his biography on Goodreads indicates that his mother was Polish and the father was a Ukranian Cossack (and he also wrote in both Russian and Ukrainian, though no doubt the languages are little more than a dialect of each other – though I would not be running around Maiden Square screaming that at this point in time)</span>
  <span id="freeText10950761547058553325" style="display:none"><b>Corrupting the Official</b><br>3 March 2014<br><br>	To start off I feel that suggesting that Gogol is a Russian would be a bit of an insult to the Ukrainians, especially at this point in time, since his biography on Goodreads indicates that his mother was Polish and the father was a Ukranian Cossack (and he also wrote in both Russian and Ukrainian, though no doubt the languages are little more than a dialect of each other – though I would not be running around Maiden Square screaming that at this point in time).<br><br>	I did not realise that I would actually enjoy this play as much as I did before I started reading it, but within the first couple of pages I suddenly discovered that the theme that Gogol works with is a very common theme in a lot of modern comedies: the idea of the mistaken identity. This is similar to the idea that comes out of Shakespeare's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/82356.The_Comedy_of_Errors" title="The Comedy of Errors by William Shakespeare" rel="nofollow noopener">A Comedy of Errors</a> (which is included in the book in which I read this particular play) however the mistaken identity does not come about because of identical twins, but rather because a visitor to a small town is mistaken for a high official who is believed to be on his way to audit the town's books.<br><br>	The play itself has been turned into a movie staring Danny Kaye (the video that I watched on Youtube has since been taken down):<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1399173561i/9518078.jpg" width="244" height="330" alt="The Inspector General – Danny Kaye" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>though there are a number of differences (both minor and major) between this film and the original play <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[(such as the main character, who is mistaken for the inspector general, becoming the new mayor to the town) <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br><br>	One sort of wonders whether Gogol is actually being critical of the corrupt bureaucracy of the Russian Empire at the time (though it is interesting to note that even some of the most advanced states in the world are open to corruption, though sometimes the corruption is much more subtle). What we have is a bunch of corrupt officials who have effectively bought their way into office and by the strength of their wealth are able to remain entrenched in that position. However, they are then warned that an inspector general from St Petersburg is coming to perform an audit on the town's finances.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1399173561i/9518079._SX540_.jpg" width="349" height="262" alt="St Petersberg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>	This sends the council into a tailspin because, well, they know if the books are audited then they are going to get found out and are probably going to face the chopping block. However, when a low level government employee arrives in the town the council, who have no idea what this inspector general looks like, immediately mistake this guy for the general and begin to court (and bribe) him in an attempt to get him to turn a blind eye. Obviously, having all this food and money thrown at him (especially since he has none of his own) encourages him to keep up the charade (though when he is caught out he promptly disappears from the town).<br><br>	I'm not all that sure if Gogol really meant to be very deep in this play because on the face of it it looks like your standard comedy, and one that works well. Further, this is one of those plays that could easily be lifted out of this current setting and placed in any other number of settings and it will still hold true (the movie places it in the Austro-hungarian empire, though it does get confusing with the suggestion that Napoleon is the emperor, when that was not the case). What it is also suggesting is that the central beauracracy is actually not all that corrupt because the people on the fringes fear their interference, and it is also suggested that corruption becomes more evident the further out you go, though in this modern world there are many that believe that corruption exists within our central halls of power and as you go out to the fringes, the corruption begins to diminish.</["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="10950761547058553325" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Jayanth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6388515-jayanth"><img alt="Jayanth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514739444p2/6388515.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer5573695646286950530">This play is a satire on the rampant corruption in governments and the public adminstration offices. I found it pretty funny.<br/><br/>A town full of neck-deep corrupt officials come to know that the government was sending an Inspector general to their town for inspection. They are scared shitless and everyone scrambles to fix and cover-up their corruption before the inspector arrives. <br/><br/>One of these gang of officials notices a man in an inn refusing to pay his bills and he ASSUMES that only a high standing</span>
  <span id="freeText5573695646286950530" style="display:none">This play is a satire on the rampant corruption in governments and the public adminstration offices. I found it pretty funny.<br /><br />A town full of neck-deep corrupt officials come to know that the government was sending an Inspector general to their town for inspection. They are scared shitless and everyone scrambles to fix and cover-up their corruption before the inspector arrives. <br /><br />One of these gang of officials notices a man in an inn refusing to pay his bills and he ASSUMES that only a high standing official from the capital can be so adamant and get away with not paying bills, while infact, the man was not paying the bills because he had no money. The official runs and passes the information that the inspector has arrived. What follows is every corrupt official trying to soften up the pretend-inspector who plunders them for his own ends.</span>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1818658484?book_show_action=true">Nov 23, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Debbie Zapata" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Debbie Zapata" href="/user/show/22110057-debbie-zapata">Debbie Zapata</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22110057-debbie-zapata?shelf=gutenberg">gutenberg</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1818658484" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6910432907533320394">I was looking forward to this play, but although the translator's introduction praised it to the skies, I ended up not being able to get too interested. It may have shown the standard operating procedure of the corrupt Russian officials of the day, and it certainly was funny at times, but it was also predictable.<br/><br/>When I read something and think 'oh, this or that is going to happen', I write down the idea and then see how long it takes to come true, if at all. This time I was right on every count.</span>
  <span id="freeText6910432907533320394" style="display:none">I was looking forward to this play, but although the translator's introduction praised it to the skies, I ended up not being able to get too interested. It may have shown the standard operating procedure of the corrupt Russian officials of the day, and it certainly was funny at times, but it was also predictable.<br /><br />When I read something and think 'oh, this or that is going to happen', I write down the idea and then see how long it takes to come true, if at all. This time I was right on every count. Mistaken identity, swindling, faked romance, expected ending, the works. <br /><br />Gogol may have been The Author of his day, but I skimmed through the last three acts in this five act play: I was bored. However, I do want to read a couple of his short story collections that are available at Project Gutenberg, so I won't give up on the man completely just yet.</span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Salma Bk" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Salma Bk" href="/user/show/22851385-salma-bk">Salma Bk</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22851385-salma-bk?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22851385-salma-bk?shelf=plays">plays</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22851385-salma-bk?shelf=russian-literature">russian-literature</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3129035826" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11743945938752004749">A very entertaining play based on the irresponsibility of heads of government who was mistaken about the identity of an inspector. They did everything to prevent him from taking into account their lack of responsibility. This fake inspector used their stupidity and allowed himself to take from each a very large sum of money and all the services he wanted. Gogol highlights in a very entertaining setting how governors and heads of public authorities are only interested in their personal interests.</span>
  <span id="freeText11743945938752004749" style="display:none">A very entertaining play based on the irresponsibility of heads of government who was mistaken about the identity of an inspector. They did everything to prevent him from taking into account their lack of responsibility. This fake inspector used their stupidity and allowed himself to take from each a very large sum of money and all the services he wanted. Gogol highlights in a very entertaining setting how governors and heads of public authorities are only interested in their personal interests.<br /><br /><b>GOVERNOR (furiously). Engaged! Bosh! A fig for your "engaged"! Confound your<br />engagement! (In desperation.) Look at me, look—all the world, all Christendom, all of you<br />see how the Governor's fooled! Ass! Booby! Dotard that I am! (Shakes his fists at himself.)<br />Ah, you fat-nose! Taking an icicle, a rag, for a man of rank! And now he's rattling along the<br />road with his bells, and telling the whole world the story! Not only do you get made a<br />laughing-stock of, but some quill-driver, some paper-stainer will go and put you in a play! It's<br />maddening! He'll spare neither your rank nor your calling, and all will grin and clap their<br />hands. . . . Who are you laughing at? laugh at yourselves . . . Ah ! you . . . (Stamps on the<br />ground ferociously.) I would do for all the pack of scribblers! Ugh! The quill-splitters!<br />Damned liberals! Devil's brood! I would scrag you all, I'd grind you to powder! You'd be a<br />dish for the foul fiend, and the devil's cap your resting-place! (Shakes his fist and grinds his<br />heel on the ground. Then, after a short silence :) I can't collect myself yet. It's true, that if God<br />will punish a man, he first drives him mad. To be sure, what was there like a Revizor in that<br />crack-brained trifler? Nothing at all! Not the resemblance of half a little finger—and all of<br />them shout at once: the Revizor, the Revizor! Who was it then who first gave out he was the<br />Revizor? Answer me!</b><br />This latest reply from the governor who became aware of his unforgivable acts is very touching and significant, the literature in each country and each century describes the situation of peoples and how they suffered under totalitarian and authoritarian systems.<br /></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2436438131" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2436438131" />
    <a title="Alan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5650849-alan"><img alt="Alan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1310234599p2/5650849.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2436438131?book_show_action=true">Jun 26, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alan" href="/user/show/5650849-alan">Alan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2436438131" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer360882468407135433"> I taught his Dead Souls a few times, but have just re-read this, a book I bought at a Princeton postdoc seminar in 1978, with the noted Russian scholar Kathryn Szczepanska (Hunter Coll) in it.<br/><br/> Gogol's comedy satirizes the ranks of Czarist government, where a “federal” inspector is rumored to be looking into this rural town, and the fearful residents seize on a slim college-age gambler passing through as the great authority because he exhibits classy manners from St Petersburg. Hilarious because</span>
  <span id="freeText360882468407135433" style="display:none"> I taught his Dead Souls a few times, but have just re-read this, a book I bought at a Princeton postdoc seminar in 1978, with the noted Russian scholar Kathryn Szczepanska (Hunter Coll) in it.<br /><br /> Gogol's comedy satirizes the ranks of Czarist government, where a “federal” inspector is rumored to be looking into this rural town, and the fearful residents seize on a slim college-age gambler passing through as the great authority because he exhibits classy manners from St Petersburg. Hilarious because so precisely observed from life: the “types,” first, the unimaginative Superintendent of Schools, Luka; 2) the Judge, named “Bungle-Steal,” who has read six books, a bit of a Freethinker, who lets the courtroom janitors raise geese in his court;3) the Town Manager/ Police Chief, center of the play, takes bribes (of course, but he asserts mostly hunting dog puppies) and worries about complaints; 4) the simple Postmaster, who opens every letter, out of traditional curiosity and caution; and 5) the Supervisor of Charitable Institutions (the hospital and prison), corpulent and awkward, but still a schemer. Lower on the hierarchy of power, Tweedle-dee and Tweedle-dum, Bobchinsky and Dubchinsky, both fat gesticulators; various functionaries and cops, like the one who’s sent into the suburbs to break up a fight, and comes back near sunrise drunk. Boozing or alcoholism features as a running theme, along with Bribery or Corruption. <br />In the last page the Police Chief recognizes he has made a complete fool of himself, but blames the tattling young Whiper-Snapper who “skims along the road with his bells jingling! He’ll spread the story all over the earth!” Gogol’s Chichikov in Dead Souls, six years later, travels the vast country in a tax scam, amassing peasants and wealth from illiterates. (Other traveller-satirists: Faulkner’s Ratliffe in his trilogy, as well as Tom Sawyer.) The Police Chief, on his rung in the top-down government, compares the young tale-teller to journalists, “You quill-drivers, you damned Liberals! you devil’s breed”(230). <br /> Talk about continuing relevance. And get this: Gogol is from Ukraine, where the college he attended now bears his name, a Nizhyn Gogol State University. If Manaford had been studying Gogol in Ukraine, I would be his chief supporter; but curiously, “plus ça change” Putin’s Russia has the same top-down hierarchy of Czarist, and even Soviet Russia.<br /><br /> Wonderfully, as the Police Chief’s wife looks forward to the Capital City, she anticipates “all sorts of delicate soups” and I was told decades ago that beside blintzes, Russian cuisine has only two high points, bread and soup. In fact, in first year Russian we were taught, ordering food, to order soup: Я хочу супу (soupu).<br /> When deciding how to bribe the young Inspector, direct money may be frowned on, so “how about an offering from the nobility for a monument of some kind?” Then the Postmaster offers, “here is some money left unclaimed at the Post Office”(199). Think of the US fight over Confederate monuments today, and of course the tearing down of Soviet monuments by Eastern European cities free from Moscow’s yoke—for how much longer?<br /><br /> The issue of memorializing Confederate White Supremacy brings us to the caution that Gogol, as arguably Shakespeare, assumes a casual anti-Semitism. Probably both derive from ignorance, having met precious few Jews—whom I must add, have formed most of my lifelong friends. Additionally, with Gogol, a comic master has to build on common assumptions. (Occasionally Gogol contradicts himself on this, noting that the anti-Police Chief complaints issue from the illiterate businessmen, who are Jews. Even the anti-Semitic would admit Jews are enviably literate.) <br />In one last 21st C US parallel, Gogol was converted by an Elder, to see his comic writing as sin. He burned most of the MS to his Dead Souls, part 2 (Purgatory, on the model of Dante's Divina Commedia.) The house where he burned his MS still stands, in Moscow.<br /> <br /> And like Griboyedov, Gogol died young, only eight years older, at 42, largely of self-induced ascetic malnourishment. </span>
  <a data-text-id="360882468407135433" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1724537166" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1724537166" />
    <a title="Steven" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4452312-steven"><img alt="Steven" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1449093910p2/4452312.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1724537166?book_show_action=true">Aug 10, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven" href="/user/show/4452312-steven">Steven</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780413733405" href="/book/show/142268.Three_Plays">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4452312-steven?shelf=russian">russian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4452312-steven?shelf=plays">plays</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4452312-steven?shelf=books-i-own">books-i-own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4452312-steven?shelf=literature">literature</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1724537166" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9826941091520197668">I had read all of Gogol's fiction, except for his plays, which were hard to find. Recently, a wonderful edition of <i>The Inspector</i> was published by Theatre Communications Group and translated by none other than Pevear and Volokhonsky. I immediately bought and read it (as all of Gogol's works, it was wonderful). Gogol also wrote two other plays, though - Marriage and The Gamblers - which I just couldn't find in a properly translated English edition. Then I stumbled on this collection, published by </span>
  <span id="freeText9826941091520197668" style="display:none">I had read all of Gogol's fiction, except for his plays, which were hard to find. Recently, a wonderful edition of <i>The Inspector</i> was published by Theatre Communications Group and translated by none other than Pevear and Volokhonsky. I immediately bought and read it (as all of Gogol's works, it was wonderful). Gogol also wrote two other plays, though - Marriage and The Gamblers - which I just couldn't find in a properly translated English edition. Then I stumbled on this collection, published by Methuen Drama (never heard of it before, to be honest). Re-reading The Government Inspector in a different translation can never hurt (this is always the case for translated works, of course - the more translations you read, the better), while I finally got to read Gogol's other plays to complete my reading of his fiction.<br /><br />This edition has a good introduction and is nicely printed (i.e., it has a good 'feel,' a nice textual layout, and is without errors). <i>Marriage</i> was had me laughing out loud in several instances - it's pure Gogol. <i>The Gamblers</i> was not quite as good, but it was still intriguing with its plot twist near the end (although, in all fairness, I saw it coming - and would expect most people to see it as well). </span>
  <a data-text-id="9826941091520197668" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_283722595" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/283722595" />
    <a title="Simon Mcleish" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7915393-simon-mcleish"><img alt="Simon Mcleish" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1347099463p2/7915393.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/283722595?book_show_action=true">Feb 25, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Simon Mcleish" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Simon Mcleish" href="/user/show/7915393-simon-mcleish">Simon Mcleish</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781854591746" href="/book/show/1066256.The_Government_Inspector">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7915393-simon-mcleish?shelf=owned">owned</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer283722595" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1926615544382990507">Originally published on my blog <a href='http://simonsbookblog.blogspot.co.uk/2000/12/nikolai-gogol-government-inspector-1836.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>here</a> in December 2000.<br/><br/>Gogol's most famous legacy may be this farce about corruption and stupidity in the local government in Tsarist Russia, but he was in fact not a political radical. He even wrote an attack on the abolition of serfdom, and spent much of his life embarrassed by the enthusiasm with which would be reformers reacted to the play. He proposed allegorical interpretations of what he had written, which would have lessened its impact if anyone had accepte</span>
  <span id="freeText1926615544382990507" style="display:none">Originally published on my blog <a href="http://simonsbookblog.blogspot.co.uk/2000/12/nikolai-gogol-government-inspector-1836.html" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a> in December 2000.<br /><br />Gogol's most famous legacy may be this farce about corruption and stupidity in the local government in Tsarist Russia, but he was in fact not a political radical. He even wrote an attack on the abolition of serfdom, and spent much of his life embarrassed by the enthusiasm with which would be reformers reacted to the play. He proposed allegorical interpretations of what he had written, which would have lessened its impact if anyone had accepted them.<br /><br />The plot, suggested by Pushkin, is fairly simple. The small, nameless, provincial town in which the play is set has discovered that the imperial government is sending an inspector, incognito, to look into the efficiency and probity of its institutions. Since the whole of the town's administration is corrupt, from the mayor and judge to the hospital, this causes consternation. When they hear of a young man from St Petersburg who is staying in one of the town's inns, they assume that he must be the inspector. They flatter and bribe him, but of course the joke is that he is not really the inspector at all. Shorn of its satirical elements, this plot was reused in an episode of Fawlty Towers; it's potential for farce is huge.<br /><br />It is possible, of course, to attack the way in which a system is abused without wanting to destroy the system itself, but I think that it is more likely that Gogol got carried away. He was not the only one, either; the Tsar himself intervened to speed up the processes of the censorship office so that the first production of The Government Inspector could go ahead remarkably quickly. (He was the most liberal Tsar Russia ever had, and he clearly had a sense of humour.) However, aspects of the play must amount to an attack on the local governmental institutions themselves. The system - or more widespread corruption within the system - was after responsible for the appointment of such an appalling group of officials in the first place.<br /><br />The reason I have for thinking that Gogol got caught up in the comedy of his theme into actually writing a radical satire is that the names of the officials are derogatory terms such as "slapdash". My favourites are just silly; the not very bright characters Bobchinsky and Dobchinsky remind an English speaking reader of Tweedledum and Tweedledee by more than just their names. Towards the end of his life, Gogol also wrote something which implies more or less what I have been suggesting, that he wanted to ridicule all that was bad in Russia, but the laughter this produced was unexpectedly overwhelming.<br /><br />Nineteenth century Russian literature has a reputation for being profound and a little dull. The Government Inspector is neither. It is extremely funny, and that has ensured its survival.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1926615544382990507" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2965272446" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2965272446" />
    <a title="Geoff Wooldridge" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/34421802-geoff-wooldridge"><img alt="Geoff Wooldridge" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1409299339p2/34421802.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2965272446?book_show_action=true">Sep 08, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Geoff Wooldridge" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Geoff Wooldridge" href="/user/show/34421802-geoff-wooldridge">Geoff Wooldridge</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781840025835" href="/book/show/198621.The_Government_Inspector">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2965272446" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12031245086656612762">The Government Inspector by Nikolay Gogol is a short, amusing play on one of Gogol's favourite subjects - the incompetence and idiocy of Russian provincial bureaucracy.<br/><br/>Set in a small town, the play features the town's Mayor, Judge, Chief of Police, Inspector of Schools, Warden of Charities and various other local officials, shopkeepers and citizens. It is rumoured that a government inspector is coming to town to prepare a report for the central government in St Petersburg, and these officials, w</span>
  <span id="freeText12031245086656612762" style="display:none">The Government Inspector by Nikolay Gogol is a short, amusing play on one of Gogol's favourite subjects - the incompetence and idiocy of Russian provincial bureaucracy.<br /><br />Set in a small town, the play features the town's Mayor, Judge, Chief of Police, Inspector of Schools, Warden of Charities and various other local officials, shopkeepers and citizens. It is rumoured that a government inspector is coming to town to prepare a report for the central government in St Petersburg, and these officials, who are all complicit in corruption, nepotism and abuse of office mistake a travelling scoundrel for the said Government Inspector.<br /><br />In a manner that is timeless in comedies involving mistaken identity, these local rogues go out of their way to curry favour with this scoundrel, who quickly realises what is happening and exploits the situation to his maximum advantage.<br /><br />All very predictable of course, but highly amusing to the point of slap-stick farce, this is typical of Gogol and a lot of fun.<br /><br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="12031245086656612762" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2757682648" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2757682648" />
    <a title="Rose" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/75007576-rose"><img alt="Rose" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603216002p2/75007576.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2757682648?book_show_action=true">Mar 30, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rose" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rose" href="/user/show/75007576-rose">Rose</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9781559367745" href="/book/show/19535666-the-inspector">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/75007576-rose?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/75007576-rose?shelf=in-translation">in-translation</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2757682648" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1095959558114740661">Swindlers swindling swindlers.<br/><br/>The corrupt officials of a provincial Russian town fear exposure and imprisonment when a government inspector comes to visit. They resolve to do everything in their power to please him and hide their mismanagement. Which basically means wining and dining him, offering him "loans", and being sycophantic toads.<br/><br/>Only, the man on the receiving end of all this attention is not an inspector at all. He's Khlestakov, a wastrel of a bureaucrat from Petersburg, who happens to </span>
  <span id="freeText1095959558114740661" style="display:none">Swindlers swindling swindlers.<br /><br />The corrupt officials of a provincial Russian town fear exposure and imprisonment when a government inspector comes to visit. They resolve to do everything in their power to please him and hide their mismanagement. Which basically means wining and dining him, offering him "loans", and being sycophantic toads.<br /><br />Only, the man on the receiving end of all this attention is not an inspector at all. He's Khlestakov, a wastrel of a bureaucrat from Petersburg, who happens to stranded in town after losing all of his money gambling. He is only too happy to encourage this misunderstanding and tells outrageous lies to inflate his imaginary position. (That famous play you're reading? He wrote it! Using a penname of course. He's also best friends with Pushkin. And it's so annoying when all the civil servants in Petersburg beg him to run their departments because he's the only one smart enough. He dines with generals and counts and holds dozens of balls because he is Very Important.)<br /><br />It's a satire on corruption, maybe. But mostly a comedy about terrible leaders who run a town like one would run a racket, and are so afraid of being caught that they fall for city manners, nice clothes, and pretty lies. There are a lot of funny lines but my personal favorites always involve the Postmaster who compulsively reads everyone's mail.<br /><br />I think comedy is the hardest to translate, especially in something dialogue heavy like a play. This isn't laugh out loud funny, at least to me reading in English. But if you like dry political satire like <i> Yes, Minister</i> then this is probably the 19th century Russian literary equivalent.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1095959558114740661" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2708132151" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2708132151" />
    <a title="Lily " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/56223415-lily"><img alt="Lily " src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

<div id="review_963343887" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/963343887" />
    <a title="Realini" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7873218-realini"><img alt="Realini" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1420269105p2/7873218.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/963343887?book_show_action=true">Jun 10, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Realini" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Realini" href="/user/show/7873218-realini">Realini</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer963343887" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1780813570274223585">The Inspector General by Nikolai Gogol<br/><br/>This is outrageously funny, but with a twist. You have all the good time in the world reading or listening to it, but you might also feel pity for the people of the world who live with such administrators and crooks.<br/>I know that one of my eyes cried with laughter and the other with bitterness.<br/>The regime I have known was communist…corrupt to the bone by definition. This was replaced, and I played my small “revolutionary” part, being mentioned in Newsweek, on a</span>
  <span id="freeText1780813570274223585" style="display:none">The Inspector General by Nikolai Gogol<br /><br />This is outrageously funny, but with a twist. You have all the good time in the world reading or listening to it, but you might also feel pity for the people of the world who live with such administrators and crooks.<br />I know that one of my eyes cried with laughter and the other with bitterness.<br />The regime I have known was communist…corrupt to the bone by definition. This was replaced, and I played my small “revolutionary” part, being mentioned in Newsweek, on about 12 (small) lines. But the system we have in place now, albeit way better than the one before, produces corruption like there is a huge conveyer belt, wherever there are public money, contracts and somehow, instead of reaching the infrastructure, health care projects they end up lining the pockets of big “wise guys” and politicians.<br />One recent scandal had one of our Forbes richest embezzling money by cheating on …cancer patients.<br />This makes the characters of The Inspector General seem not only funny, but somewhat ridiculous by comparison. After all, they only talk about a church that was supposed to be erected with public money, and never started…and a few other petty misdemeanors.<br />In the production that I listened to this morning, on my morning run, the title role is played by Beligan. They say he is the oldest actor still acting on stage…in the world.<br />I respect that, but I am no fan and have never been. To be honest, the way he speaks makes him funny, but not always in comedies, even in tragic roles. And I am not talking about now, when we could give him a break…come on, he is ninety five!<br />The play I listened to was some 60 years old; it had Giugaru and some other actors who had been long dead before I was born.<br />In The Inspector General (Revizorul for us) there is a kerfuffle generated by a tipoff: be careful my friend, an official is coming. The entire region is on fire and picks up the wrong man. They mistake a small time crook for the high ranking official and begin to pour generous meals, money on him.<br />The inspector enjoys it…up to a point.<br />Life can be better than fiction: a majority of the Forbes richest have been arrested and/or are on trial for corruption, embezzlement, tax evasion …you name it.<br />Local officials, like our heroes in The Inspector General, have been placed behind bars, after inquests which seem to be a combination of Gogol and The American Hustle.<br />Some of these elected (a shortcoming of uneducated people voting) officials go to dance samba in Brazil, have exorbitant life styles all on tax payers money, who…keep on voting for them.<br />“How silly can you get” …was a line in a song from the comedy Top Secret, with Val Kilmer. When we were teenagers we watched that movie so much, we knew the lines backwards.<br /><br />“Watch this one; this will make you very popular!!”<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.realini.blogspot.ro">www.realini.blogspot.ro</a> <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1780813570274223585" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2420970412" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2420970412" />
    <a title="Charles Puskas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/388830-charles-puskas"><img alt="Charles Puskas" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1190166703p2/388830.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2420970412?book_show_action=true">Jun 11, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Charles Puskas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Charles Puskas" href="/user/show/388830-charles-puskas">Charles Puskas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2420970412" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11241577857360250381">Considered the high point of Gogol's writing for the stage & a masterpiece of dramatic satire (writes Vladimir Nabokov), The Inspector General skewers the stupidity, greed, & venality of Russian provincial officials. When it is announced that the Inspector General is coming to visit incognito, Anton, the Mayor, hastens to clean up the town before his arrival. Local officials (police chief, supt. of schools, postmaster) scurry to hide evidence of bribe-taking & other misdeeds, setting the stage f</span>
  <span id="freeText11241577857360250381" style="display:none">Considered the high point of Gogol's writing for the stage &amp; a masterpiece of dramatic satire (writes Vladimir Nabokov), The Inspector General skewers the stupidity, greed, &amp; venality of Russian provincial officials. When it is announced that the Inspector General is coming to visit incognito, Anton, the Mayor, hastens to clean up the town before his arrival. Local officials (police chief, supt. of schools, postmaster) scurry to hide evidence of bribe-taking &amp; other misdeeds, setting the stage for the arrival from St. Petersburg of young Ivan, a penurious gambler &amp; rake who (because of his haughty behavior) is promptly taken by the townspeople to be the dreaded Inspector General. Ivan, and his servant, Osip, soon take advantage of the situation (free room &amp; board, monetary "loans," etc.) with hilarious results. First performed in 1836, the play transcends regional &amp; national boundaries to offer a biting, highly entertaining glimpse of universal human foibles &amp; failings.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11241577857360250381" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Ellie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1787638-ellie"><img alt="Ellie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1232303617p2/1787638.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ellie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ellie" href="/user/show/1787638-ellie">Ellie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780413733405" href="/book/show/142268.Three_Plays">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer42214290" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4700597215908780796">The Government Inspector is one of my favourite plays of all time.<br/><br/><br/>It is satire at its absolute best; it shows the problems at every level of society, every occupation and without any worry for what people might think.<br/><br/>Set in a small provincial government town who are expecting an inspector to arrive any day, a young man who is silly and broke, travelling back to his parents estate to ask for money.<br/><br/>He arrives in the town and is mistaken for the Government Inspector. The story unfolds from there, b</span>
  <span id="freeText4700597215908780796" style="display:none">The Government Inspector is one of my favourite plays of all time.<br /><br /><br />It is satire at its absolute best; it shows the problems at every level of society, every occupation and without any worry for what people might think.<br /><br />Set in a small provincial government town who are expecting an inspector to arrive any day, a young man who is silly and broke, travelling back to his parents estate to ask for money.<br /><br />He arrives in the town and is mistaken for the Government Inspector. The story unfolds from there, but it is brilliantly funny and clever.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4700597215908780796" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1194924119" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1194924119" />
    <a title="Bruce" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/897874-bruce"><img alt="Bruce" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1202929351p2/897874.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1194924119?book_show_action=true">Feb 08, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bruce" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bruce" href="/user/show/897874-bruce">Bruce</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9785050024374" href="/book/show/1327368.The_Government_Inspector_and_Selected_Stories">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">lovers of the supernatural, satire, Russian &amp; French literature</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/897874-bruce?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/897874-bruce?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1194924119" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15407784455381402980">We've had this book on our collective shelf since my wife picked it up at a bookstore in (then) Leningrad (now St. Petersburg). She was looking for something in English to read to break up the monotony of a Soviet semester abroad. I feel her pain, as I was coincidentally spending a similar semester in the starving, stony wastes of corrupt, collapsing Moscow.<br/><br/>My impression of Gogol was always loosely dependent on Shostakovich's adaptations of <i>The Nose</i> and <i>The Gamblers</i>. In other words, I took him s</span>
  <span id="freeText15407784455381402980" style="display:none">We've had this book on our collective shelf since my wife picked it up at a bookstore in (then) Leningrad (now St. Petersburg). She was looking for something in English to read to break up the monotony of a Soviet semester abroad. I feel her pain, as I was coincidentally spending a similar semester in the starving, stony wastes of corrupt, collapsing Moscow.<br /><br />My impression of Gogol was always loosely dependent on Shostakovich's adaptations of <i>The Nose</i> and <i>The Gamblers</i>. In other words, I took him strictly as a caustic satirist of human corruption, and it struck me as more than a bit ironic for his works to be promoted (let alone accepted) in communist Russia, whose bureaucrats and party scions would be highly recognizable to Gogol's readership. If you asked me when Gogol wrote his stories, I'd have told you the turn of the 20th century. They feel that modern.<br /><br />Imagine my surprise, then, to find that these works were actually composed in the 1830's, and his actual targets the local bourgeois. This collection is almost entirely a reproduction of his <u>St. Petersburg Stories</u>, which is probably how my wife came to find it on the shelf of a beriozka off Nevsky Prospekt. I'll provide a quick synopsis of the contents, but a general stylistic observation is first in order.<br /><br />Gogol's stories and plays, at least the ones represented here, share much in common with the best of Edgar Allan Poe. The point of view tends toward the claustrophobic and supernatural, the tone bitingly satiric, the rhythm relentless. <i>The Portrait</i> is practically two stories smashed together: the first is a fable of a destitute painter whose integrity gives way to venality and envy after he gains sufficient capital to market his talents (a lucrative, if soul-killing exchange), the second, an auction-story taletelling of a masterwork's origins (the demonic effects of which readers witnessed in the first half). In this work is the resemblance of Gogol to Poe most evident.<blockquote>Through a gap in the curtain he could see his room bathed in moonlight and in it the portrait hanging stiffly on the wall. The eyes fixed upon him still in a manner both terrible and significant, seeming to scorn everything else. Overpowered by a feeling of oppression, he rose suddenly from his bed, seized a sheet, and, approaching the portrait, covered it up completely.<br /><br />After this he lay down feeling calmer and began to meditate upon the poverty and wretched lot of the true artist, of the thorny path he has to tread in life, while his eyes glanced involuntarily through the part in the screen at the muffled portrait. The moonlight accentuated the whiteness of the sheet, and it seemed to him as though those terrible eyes shone right through the cloth. In horror he stared harder as if to assure himself that this was nonsense! But eventually he saw--saw clearly; there was no longer a sheet--the portrait was quite uncovered, and was gazing beyond everything around it, straight at him; gazing as it seemed fairly into his heart. His heart froze. He watched in agony as the old man [of the portrait] moved... and... taking hold of the frame with both hands, heaved himself up... and, thrusting out both legs... leapt suddenly from the frame! Now through the gap of the screen, Chartkov could see only the empty frame. Footsteps resounded through the room, drawing closer and closer to his curtain. The poor artist's heart started pounding. More dead than alive he waited for the old man's face to appear round the screen. And lo! in due course the very same jaundiced face appeared with its large eyes roving about the room. (p. 195)</blockquote>While Gogol spoofs personalities more than impersonal systems, he must certainly be considered a progenitor of Kafka, Dostoevsky, and possibly (if transposed to the salon) Chekhov. <i>The Greatcoat</i> (more commonly known as <i>The Overcoat</i>) is as plangent as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/333538.The_Castle" title="The Castle by Franz Kafka" rel="nofollow noopener">The Castle</a> or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17690.The_Trial" title="The Trial by Franz Kafka" rel="nofollow noopener">The Trial</a>, as the dignity of a poor, pure, simple-minded scrivener is repeatedly attacked, by bullies at work, by poverty, by a drunken tailor, by thieves, and ultimately by a self-important minor dignitary who refuses to hear out his woes. Unlike similar, later tales of beset disempowered outsiders (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/890.Of_Mice_and_Men" title="Of Mice and Men by John Steinbeck" rel="nofollow noopener">Of Mice and Men</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2189470.Peter_Grimes" title="Peter Grimes by George Crabbe" rel="nofollow noopener">Peter Grimes</a> come to mind), however, Gogol cannot resist rendering this tragedy a farcical tone. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[(relating rumors that his protagonist has turned vindictive ghost, seeking in death the prize that was stolen from him in life) <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />His plots do tend to ramble. I've already mentioned the split-personality of <i>The Portrait</i>. Likewise, <i>Nevsky Prospekt</i> starts as a Mark Twain-esque travelogue snapshot of St. Petersburg's grand promenade, before animating to follow the respective skirt-chasing adventures of two men in serie, each to their respective (and deliberately opposed) climaxes. <br /><br />Even his lampoon <i>The Nose</i>, in which an arriviste's ambitions are briefly upstaged by his own nasal appendage run amok, transforms itself from a character lampoon of a sotted barber into more <u>Metamorphosis</u> territory when it lights upon the barber's temporary victim: "'The point is, you're my very own nose!'… 'You are mistaken, my good sir; I am myself. Besides... to judge by the buttons of your uniform, you must be in quite a different department to mine.' So saying, the nose turned away." (at page 165, bang! The nose snub, revolver.) Reading Gogol, you get the sense of the author coming up with a better idea mid-story without bothering to revise what he's already put on paper. The beneficial end result is a feeling of disequilibrium, at once fluid and unpredictable.<br /><br />Well, not wholly… the play from which this collection takes its name, <i>The Government Inspector</i>, is about what you'd expect, a straight, one-act farce -- its five "acts" are really just separate scenes -- about a bunch of trick-card playing provincials who mistakenly throw their largess on an unwitting imposter. The monologuing gets a tad long-winded for the merriment, but this could play as a French boulevardier, with none the wiser. In fact, Danny Kaye starred in a wonderfully silly adaptation, that remains very true to the spirit of the original.<br /><br />Amongst this august company, <i>Diary of a Madman</i> appears as the least of this collection, a theme and variations frolic that relies on a journal-style format with ominous (if thickly veiled) allusions to the "treatment" routinely administered to the mentally ill of the mid-19th century. There's little more to be said here. Not enough sex or scatology for the prurient-minded, but if you are but a minor cog whose romantic aspirations are frustrated even by the literal dogs of society, then you too should share in the vicarious pleasure of shouting "I'm the Emperor of Spain!" to the rooftops. Actually, the part of this story I enjoyed most was the evolving dates… from a straightforward "October 3rd" to the odder "the 30th of Februarius," culminating in "No date. The day had no date." If nothing else, let this serve as proof of the timelessness of Gogol's art.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15407784455381402980" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText10758706100502386084" style="display:none">actual rating: *2.00 stars*<br /><br />there is nothing worse than being forced to read by your literature professor, especially when the book you are supposed to read is not. good. this is a comedy from realism, written in a form of tragedy — but nonetheless, it made me want to cry from boredom. it’s quite confusing at certain parts, the theme of a rotten society and corruption has been overused by all classics authors in previous epochs, and to put it simply: this is a piece in which the ideals and themes were executed poorly, so it is hard to connect to this and the plot was awful. <br /><br />this is why teenagers back away from reading; they are forced to read books like these and then they assume every other book is the same. tragic. </span>

<div id="review_3183928367" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3183928367" />
    <a title="Clementine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/31037758-clementine"><img alt="Clementine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1419176916p2/31037758.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3183928367?book_show_action=true">Jun 18, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Clementine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Clementine" href="/user/show/31037758-clementine">Clementine</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9782080704979" href="/book/show/1923532.Le_Revizor">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31037758-clementine?shelf=russian-snow">russian-snow</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31037758-clementine?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31037758-clementine?shelf=play">play</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31037758-clementine?shelf=classic-study">classic-study</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3183928367" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3391693313624965010">I've read a few comedy plays with a tragic twist, but I felt this one fell short. The misunderstandings piled up, and it was funny, so the comic aspects were there. I simply didn't get a good enough sense of the main character's mischievous and immoral ways to truly appreciate how and when he got to the point of taking advantage of everyone. The reveal at the end fell flat for the same reason, while completely predictable. People are shocked in the last scene, but there was no real consequence e</span>
  <span id="freeText3391693313624965010" style="display:none">I've read a few comedy plays with a tragic twist, but I felt this one fell short. The misunderstandings piled up, and it was funny, so the comic aspects were there. I simply didn't get a good enough sense of the main character's mischievous and immoral ways to truly appreciate how and when he got to the point of taking advantage of everyone. The reveal at the end fell flat for the same reason, while completely predictable. People are shocked in the last scene, but there was no real consequence except for their monetary loss. Plus, they talked it over for a whole scene, so when the real "revizor" shows up in the following scene and everyone is shook? But, whyyy? We knew this would happen. <br /><br />I've often been told that Russian plays' strengths lie in their subtlety. Maybe I missed something here because the text was translated? Anyway, this was enjoyable, right until the end. It simply wasn't poignant for me. </span>

<div id="review_2184723092" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2184723092" />
    <a title="Gary" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18697926-gary"><img alt="Gary" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1559281739p2/18697926.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2184723092?book_show_action=true">Nov 14, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gary" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gary" href="/user/show/18697926-gary">Gary</a>

        rated it

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer2594547879840015875">I read this play in college while studying in the school of Drama. It took me no less then ten seconds to recognize the play as it was described in The Skeptic's guide to the Great Books. I had forgotten that I read it and it was an oversight. I loved this play and it's hilarity. Now, I have to find a copy and reread it so I can write a proper review. </span>

          </span>
